     <p><b>Harvard University</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Private_university" title="Private university">private</a> <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> <a href="/wiki/Doctoral_university" title="Doctoral university" class="mw-redirect">research university</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>. It was established in 1636 by the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_General_Court" title="Massachusetts General Court">Massachusetts legislature</a>, being the <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Colleges" title="Colonial Colleges" class="mw-redirect">oldest</a> institution of higher learning<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> and the first <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">corporation</a> (officially <i>The President and Fellows of Harvard College</i>) chartered in the country. Its history, quality of education, academic influence, and wealth have made it one of the most prestigious universities in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Harvard was named after its first benefactor, <a href="/wiki/John_Harvard_(clergyman)" title="John Harvard (clergyman)">John Harvard</a>. Although never formally affiliated with a church, the college primarily trained <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregationalist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> clergy. Harvard's curriculum and students became secular throughout the 18th century and by the 19th century had emerged as the central cultural establishment among <a href="/wiki/Boston_Brahmin" title="Boston Brahmin">Boston elites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, President <a href="/wiki/Charles_W._Eliot" title="Charles W. Eliot" class="mw-redirect">Charles W. Eliot</a>'s forty-year tenure (1869?1909) transformed the college and affiliated professional schools into a centralized <a href="/wiki/Research_university" title="Research university" class="mw-redirect">research university</a>, and Harvard became a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a> in 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-AAU_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAU-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Bryant_Conant" title="James Bryant Conant">James Bryant Conant</a> led the university through the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and began to reform the curriculum and liberalize admissions after the war. The undergraduate college became coeducational after its 1977 merger with <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_College" title="Radcliffe College">Radcliffe College</a>. <a href="/wiki/Drew_Gilpin_Faust" title="Drew Gilpin Faust">Drew Gilpin Faust</a> was elected the 28th president in 2007 and is the first woman to lead the university. Harvard has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_colleges_and_universities_in_the_United_States_by_endowment" title="List of colleges and universities in the United States by endowment">largest</a> <a href="/wiki/Financial_endowment" title="Financial endowment">financial endowment</a> of any academic institution in the world, standing at $30.4 billion as of September 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-NACUBO_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NACUBO-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university comprises eleven separate academic units?ten faculties and the <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_Institute_for_Advanced_Study" title="Radcliffe Institute for Advanced Study">Radcliffe Institute for Advanced Study</a>?with campuses throughout the Boston metropolitan area.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Harvard's 209-acre (85&nbsp;ha) main campus is centered on <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Yard" title="Harvard Yard">Harvard Yard</a> in Cambridge, approximately 3.4 miles (5.5&nbsp;km) northwest of downtown <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Business_School" title="Harvard Business School">business school</a> and athletics facilities, including <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Stadium" title="Harvard Stadium">Harvard Stadium</a>, are located across the <a href="/wiki/Charles_River" title="Charles River">Charles River</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Allston" title="Allston">Allston</a> neighborhood of Boston and the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Medical_School" title="Harvard Medical School">medical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_School_of_Dental_Medicine" title="Harvard School of Dental Medicine">dental</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_School_of_Public_Health" title="Harvard School of Public Health">public health</a> schools are located in the <a href="/wiki/Longwood_Medical_and_Academic_Area" title="Longwood Medical and Academic Area">Longwood Medical Area</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Campus_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campus-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Eight <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States_by_education" title="List of Presidents of the United States by education">U.S. presidents</a> have been graduates, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_by_university_affiliation" title="List of Nobel laureates by university affiliation">141 Nobel Laureates</a> have been student, faculty, or staff affiliates. Harvard is also the <a href="/wiki/Alma_mater" title="Alma mater">alma mater</a> of sixty-two living <a href="/wiki/Billionaires" title="Billionaires" class="mw-redirect">billionaires</a>, the most in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Library" title="Harvard University Library">Harvard University Library</a> is the largest academic library in the United States, and one of the largest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-largestlibs_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-largestlibs-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Crimson" title="Harvard Crimson">Harvard Crimson</a> competes in 42 intercollegiate sports in the <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">NCAA</a> <a href="/wiki/Division_I_(NCAA)" title="Division I (NCAA)">Division I</a> <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a>. Harvard has an intense athletic rivalry with <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> culminating in <i><a href="/wiki/Harvard%E2%80%93Yale_football_rivalry" title="Harvard?Yale football rivalry">The Game</a></i>, although the <a href="/wiki/Harvard%E2%80%93Yale_Regatta" title="Harvard?Yale Regatta">Harvard?Yale Regatta</a> predates the football game. This rivalry, though, is put aside every two years when the Harvard and Yale <a href="/wiki/Track_and_Field" title="Track and Field" class="mw-redirect">Track and Field</a> teams come together to compete against a combined <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" title="Oxford University" class="mw-redirect">Oxford University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" title="Cambridge University" class="mw-redirect">Cambridge University</a> team, a competition that is the oldest continuous international amateur competition in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <table id="toc" class="toc">

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_Harvard_University" title="History of Harvard University">History of Harvard University</a>
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial">Colonial</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:A_Westerly_View_of_the_Colledges_in_Cambridge_New_England_by_Paul_Revere.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/A_Westerly_View_of_the_Colledges_in_Cambridge_New_England_by_Paul_Revere.jpeg/220px-A_Westerly_View_of_the_Colledges_in_Cambridge_New_England_by_Paul_Revere.jpeg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/A_Westerly_View_of_the_Colledges_in_Cambridge_New_England_by_Paul_Revere.jpeg/330px-A_Westerly_View_of_the_Colledges_in_Cambridge_New_England_by_Paul_Revere.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/A_Westerly_View_of_the_Colledges_in_Cambridge_New_England_by_Paul_Revere.jpeg/440px-A_Westerly_View_of_the_Colledges_in_Cambridge_New_England_by_Paul_Revere.jpeg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:A_Westerly_View_of_the_Colledges_in_Cambridge_New_England_by_Paul_Revere.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Engraving of 
        <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a> by 
        <a href="/wiki/Paul_Revere" title="Paul Revere">Paul Revere</a>, 1767
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Harvard was founded in 1636 by vote of the Great and General Court of the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bay_Colony" title="Massachusetts Bay Colony">Massachusetts Bay Colony</a>, making it the oldest institution of higher learning in the United States. Initially called &quot;New College&quot; or &quot;the college at New Towne&quot;, the institution was renamed <i><a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a></i> on March 13, 1639. It was named after <a href="/wiki/John_Harvard_(clergyman)" title="John Harvard (clergyman)">John Harvard</a>, a young English <a href="/wiki/Clergyman" title="Clergyman" class="mw-redirect">clergyman</a> from <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">Southwark</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, an alumnus of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a> (after which Cambridge, Massachusetts is named), who bequeathed the College his library of four hundred books and <a href="/wiki/Pound_sign" title="Pound sign">&pound;</a>779 <a href="/wiki/Pounds_sterling" title="Pounds sterling" class="mw-redirect">pounds sterling</a>, which was half of his estate.<sup id="cite_ref-JH_facts_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JH_facts-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> The charter creating the corporation of Harvard College came in 1650. In the early years, the College trained many Puritan ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> The college offered a classic academic course based on the English university model?many leaders in the colony had attended the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>?but one consistent with the prevailing <a href="/wiki/Puritan" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> philosophy. The college was never affiliated with any particular denomination, but many of its earliest graduates went on to become clergymen in Congregational and Unitarian churches throughout New England.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> An early brochure, published in 1643, described the founding of the college as a response to the desire &quot;to advance learning and perpetuate it to posterity, dreading to leave an illiterate ministry to the churches&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The leading Boston divine <a href="/wiki/Increase_Mather" title="Increase Mather">Increase Mather</a> served as president from 1685 to 1701. In 1708, <a href="/wiki/John_Leverett_the_Younger" title="John Leverett the Younger">John Leverett</a> became the first president who was not also a clergyman, which marked a turning of the college toward intellectual independence from Puritanism.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:John_Harvard_statue_at_Harvard_University.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/John_Harvard_statue_at_Harvard_University.jpg/220px-John_Harvard_statue_at_Harvard_University.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/John_Harvard_statue_at_Harvard_University.jpg/330px-John_Harvard_statue_at_Harvard_University.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/John_Harvard_statue_at_Harvard_University.jpg/440px-John_Harvard_statue_at_Harvard_University.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:John_Harvard_statue_at_Harvard_University.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/John_Harvard_statue" title="John Harvard statue">John Harvard statue</a>, 
        <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Yard" title="Harvard Yard">Harvard Yard</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_philosophy">Religion and philosophy</span></h4> 
     <p>The takeover of Harvard by the <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarians</a> in 1805 resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">secularization</a> of the American college. By 1850 Harvard was the &quot;Unitarian Vatican&quot;. The &quot;liberals&quot; (Unitarians) allied themselves with <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">high Federalists</a> and began to create a set of private societies and institutions meant to shore up their cultural and political authority, a movement that prefigured the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Brahmin" title="Boston Brahmin">Boston Brahmin</a> class. On the other hand, the theological conservatives used print media to argue for the maintenance of open debate and democratic governance through a diverse public sphere, seeing the liberals' movement as an attempt to create a cultural oligarchy in opposition to Congregationalist tradition and republican political principles.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1846, the natural history lectures of <a href="/wiki/Louis_Agassiz" title="Louis Agassiz">Louis Agassiz</a> were acclaimed both in New York and on the campus at Harvard College. Agassiz's approach was distinctly idealist and posited Americans' &quot;participation in the Divine Nature&quot; and the possibility of understanding &quot;intellectual existences&quot;.' Agassiz's perspective on science combined observation with intuition and the assumption that a person can grasp the &quot;divine plan&quot; in all phenomena. When it came to explaining life-forms, Agassiz resorted to matters of shape based on a presumed archetype for his evidence. This dual view of knowledge was in concert with the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Common_Sense_Realism" title="Common Sense Realism" class="mw-redirect">Common Sense Realism</a> derived from Scottish philosophers <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Reid" title="Thomas Reid">Thomas Reid</a> and Dugald Stewart, whose works were part of the Harvard curriculum at the time. The popularity of Agassiz's efforts to &quot;soar with Plato&quot; probably also derived from other writings to which Harvard students were exposed, including Platonic treatises by Ralph Cudworth, John Norrisand, in a Romantic vein, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Coleridge" title="Samuel Coleridge" class="mw-redirect">Samuel Coleridge</a>. The library records at Harvard reveal that the writings of Plato and his early modern and Romantic followers were almost as regularly read during the 19th century as those of the &quot;official philosophy&quot; of the more empirical and more deistic Scottish school.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Charles_W._Eliot" title="Charles W. Eliot" class="mw-redirect">Charles W. Eliot</a>, president 1869?1909, eliminated the favored position of Christianity from the curriculum while opening it to student self-direction. While Eliot was the most crucial figure in the secularization of American higher education, he was motivated not by a desire to secularize education, but by <a href="/wiki/Transcendentalism" title="Transcendentalism">Transcendentalist</a> <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> convictions. Derived from <a href="/wiki/William_Ellery_Channing" title="William Ellery Channing">William Ellery Channing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a>, these convictions were focused on the dignity and worth of human nature, the right and ability of each person to perceive truth, and the indwelling God in each person.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Rummell,_Richard_Harvard_University.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Rummell%2C_Richard_Harvard_University.jpg/300px-Rummell%2C_Richard_Harvard_University.jpg" width="300" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Rummell%2C_Richard_Harvard_University.jpg/450px-Rummell%2C_Richard_Harvard_University.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Rummell%2C_Richard_Harvard_University.jpg/600px-Rummell%2C_Richard_Harvard_University.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Rummell,_Richard_Harvard_University.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Richard Rummell's 1906 watercolor landscape view, facing northeast.
        <sup id="cite_ref-http:.2F.2Fgrahamarader.blogspot.com.2F2011.2F07.2Ficonic-college-view-harvard-university.html_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-http:.2F.2Fgrahamarader.blogspot.com.2F2011.2F07.2Ficonic-college-view-harvard-university.html-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>During the 20th century, Harvard's international reputation grew as a burgeoning endowment and prominent professors expanded the university's scope. Explosive growth in the student population continued with the addition of new graduate schools and the expansion of the undergraduate program. <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_College" title="Radcliffe College">Radcliffe College</a>, established in 1879 as sister school of Harvard College, became one of the most prominent schools for women in the United States. Harvard became a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a> in 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-AAU_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAU-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Meritocracy">Meritocracy</span></h4> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/James_Bryant_Conant" title="James Bryant Conant">James Bryant Conant</a> (president, 1933?1953) reinvigorated creative scholarship to guarantee its preeminence among research institutions. He saw higher education as a vehicle of opportunity for the talented rather than an entitlement for the wealthy, so Conant devised programs to identify, recruit, and support talented youth. In 1943, he asked the faculty make a definitive statement about what general education ought to be, at the secondary as well as the college level. The resulting <i>Report</i>, published in 1945, was one of the most influential manifestos in the history of American education in the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1945?1960 admissions policies were opened up to bring in students from a more diverse applicant pool. No longer drawing mostly from rich alumni of select New England prep schools, the undergraduate college was now open to striving middle class students from public schools; many more Jews and Catholics were admitted, but few blacks, Hispanics or Asians.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Women">Women</span></h4> 
     <p>Women remained segregated at Radcliffe, though more and more took Harvard classes. Nonetheless, Harvard's undergraduate population remained predominantly male, with about four men attending Harvard College for every woman studying at Radcliffe. Following the merger of Harvard and Radcliffe admissions in 1977, the proportion of female undergraduates steadily increased, mirroring a trend throughout higher education in the United States. Harvard's graduate schools, which had accepted females and other groups in greater numbers even before the college, also became more diverse in the post-World War II period.</p> 
     <p>In 1999, <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_College" title="Radcliffe College">Radcliffe College</a>, founded in 1879 as the &quot;Harvard Annex for Women&quot;,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> merged formally with Harvard University, becoming the <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_Institute_for_Advanced_Study" title="Radcliffe Institute for Advanced Study">Radcliffe Institute for Advanced Study</a>.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Drew_Gilpin_Faust" title="Drew Gilpin Faust">Drew Gilpin Faust</a>, the Dean at Radcliffe, became the first woman president of Harvard in 2007.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_square_harvard_yard.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Harvard_square_harvard_yard.JPG/220px-Harvard_square_harvard_yard.JPG" width="220" height="108" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Harvard_square_harvard_yard.JPG/330px-Harvard_square_harvard_yard.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Harvard_square_harvard_yard.JPG/440px-Harvard_square_harvard_yard.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_square_harvard_yard.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Yard" title="Harvard Yard">Harvard Yard</a> as seen from the Holyoke Center
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberalism">Liberalism</span></h4> 
     <p>Harvard and its affiliates, like many American universities,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> are considered by some to be politically <a href="/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern liberalism in the United States">liberal</a> (left of center).<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">Conservative</a> author <a href="/wiki/William_F._Buckley,_Jr." title="William F. Buckley, Jr.">William F. Buckley, Jr.</a> quipped that he would rather be governed by the first 2000 names in the Boston phone book than by the Harvard faculty,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> famously referred to Harvard as the &quot;<a href="/wiki/Moscow_Kremlin" title="Moscow Kremlin">Kremlin</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Charles_River" title="Charles River">Charles</a>&quot; around 1970,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> and Vice President <a href="/wiki/George_H.W._Bush" title="George H.W. Bush" class="mw-redirect">George H.W. Bush</a> disparaged what he saw to be Harvard's liberalism during the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1988" title="United States presidential election, 1988">1988 presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Such is its reputation as a bastion of liberalism and privilege, alumni who run for public office sometimes downplay their affiliation with the university.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history">Recent history</span></h4> 
     <p>President <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Summers" title="Lawrence Summers">Lawrence Summers</a> resigned his presidency in 2006. His resignation came one week before a second planned vote of no confidence by the Harvard Faculty of Arts and Sciences. Former president <a href="/wiki/Derek_Bok" title="Derek Bok">Derek Bok</a> served as interim president. Members of Harvard's Faculty of Arts and Sciences, which instructs graduate students in GSAS and undergraduates in Harvard College, had passed an earlier motion of &quot;lack of confidence&quot; in Summers' leadership on March 15, 2005 by a 218?185 vote, with 18 abstentions. The 2005 motion was precipitated by comments about the causes of gender demographics in academia made at a closed academic conference and leaked to the press.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> In response, Summers convened two committees to study this issue: the Task Force on Women Faculty and the Task Force on Women in Science and Engineering. Summers had also pledged $50 million to support their recommendations and other proposed reforms. <a href="/wiki/Drew_Gilpin_Faust" title="Drew Gilpin Faust">Drew Gilpin Faust</a> is the 28th president of Harvard. An <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States" title="History of the United States">American historian</a>, former dean of the <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_Institute_for_Advanced_Study" title="Radcliffe Institute for Advanced Study">Radcliffe Institute for Advanced Study</a>, and Lincoln Professor of History at Harvard University, Faust is the first female president in the university's history.<sup id="cite_ref-crimson-pres_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crimson-pres-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-harvard-pres_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harvard-pres-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span></h2> 
     <p>Harvard's 209-acre (85&nbsp;ha) main campus is centered on <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Yard" title="Harvard Yard">Harvard Yard</a> in Cambridge, approximately 3.4 miles (5.5&nbsp;km) northwest of downtown <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> and extends into the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Square" title="Harvard Square">Harvard Square</a> neighborhood. Harvard Yard itself contains the central administrative offices and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Library" title="Harvard University Library">main libraries of the university</a>, academic buildings including <a href="/wiki/Sever_Hall" title="Sever Hall">Sever Hall</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_Hall_(Harvard_University)" title="University Hall (Harvard University)">University Hall</a>, Memorial Church, and the majority of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Harvard_dormitories" title="List of Harvard dormitories">freshman dormitories</a>. Sophomore, junior, and senior undergraduates live in twelve <a href="/wiki/Harvard_House_system" title="Harvard House system" class="mw-redirect">residential Houses</a>, nine of which are south of Harvard Yard along or near the <a href="/wiki/Charles_River" title="Charles River">Charles River</a>. The other three are located in a residential neighborhood half a mile northwest of the Yard at the <a href="/wiki/Quadrangle_(Harvard)" title="Quadrangle (Harvard)">Quadrangle</a> (commonly referred to as the Quad), which formerly housed <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_College" title="Radcliffe College">Radcliffe College</a> students until Radcliffe merged its residential system with Harvard. The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_(MBTA_station)" title="Harvard (MBTA station)">Harvard MBTA station</a> provides public transportation via bus service and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Line_(MBTA)" title="Red Line (MBTA)">Red Line subway</a>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_college_-_annenberg_hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Harvard_college_-_annenberg_hall.jpg/220px-Harvard_college_-_annenberg_hall.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Harvard_college_-_annenberg_hall.jpg/330px-Harvard_college_-_annenberg_hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Harvard_college_-_annenberg_hall.jpg/440px-Harvard_college_-_annenberg_hall.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_college_-_annenberg_hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Hall_(Harvard_University)" title="Memorial Hall (Harvard University)">Memorial Hall</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The Harvard Business School and many of the university's athletics facilities, including <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Stadium" title="Harvard Stadium">Harvard Stadium</a>, are located on a 358-acre (145&nbsp;ha) campus opposite the Cambridge campus in <a href="/wiki/Allston,_Massachusetts" title="Allston, Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect">Allston</a>. The <a href="/wiki/John_W._Weeks_Bridge" title="John W. Weeks Bridge">John W. Weeks Bridge</a> is a pedestrian bridge over the <a href="/wiki/Charles_River" title="Charles River">Charles River</a> connecting both campuses. The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Medical_School" title="Harvard Medical School">Harvard Medical School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_School_of_Dental_Medicine" title="Harvard School of Dental Medicine">Harvard School of Dental Medicine</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_School_of_Public_Health" title="Harvard School of Public Health">Harvard School of Public Health</a> are located on a 21-acre (8.5&nbsp;ha) campus in the <a href="/wiki/Longwood_Medical_and_Academic_Area" title="Longwood Medical and Academic Area">Longwood Medical and Academic Area</a> approximately 3.3 miles (5.3&nbsp;km) southwest of downtown Boston and 3.3 miles (5.3&nbsp;km) south of the Cambridge campus.<sup id="cite_ref-Campus_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campus-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> A private shuttle bus connects the Longwood campus to the Cambridge campus via <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Avenue_(Boston)" title="Massachusetts Avenue (Boston)">Massachusetts Avenue</a> making stops in the <a href="/wiki/Back_Bay,_Boston" title="Back Bay, Boston">Back Bay</a> and at <a href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT" class="mw-redirect">MIT</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Each residential house contains rooms for undergraduates, House masters, and resident tutors, as well as a dining hall and library. The facilities were made possible by a gift from <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> alumnus <a href="/wiki/Edward_Harkness" title="Edward Harkness">Edward Harkness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_memorial_church_winter_2009.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Harvard_memorial_church_winter_2009.JPG/170px-Harvard_memorial_church_winter_2009.JPG" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Harvard_memorial_church_winter_2009.JPG/255px-Harvard_memorial_church_winter_2009.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Harvard_memorial_church_winter_2009.JPG/340px-Harvard_memorial_church_winter_2009.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_memorial_church_winter_2009.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Memorial Church in winter
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Radcliffe Yard, formerly the center of the campus of Radcliffe College (and now home of the Radcliffe Institute), is adjacent to the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Graduate_School_of_Education" title="Harvard Graduate School of Education">Graduate School of Education</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Common" title="Cambridge Common">Cambridge Common</a>.</p> 
     <p>From 2009?2011, Harvard University reported on-campus crime statistics that included 69 forcible sex offenses, 12 robberies, 15 aggravated assaults, 80 burglaries, and 10 cases of motor vehicle theft.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellite_facilities">Satellite facilities</span></h3> 
     <p>Apart from its major Cambridge/Allston and Longwood campuses, Harvard owns and operates <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Arboretum" title="Arnold Arboretum">Arnold Arboretum</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Jamaica_Plain,_Massachusetts" title="Jamaica Plain, Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect">Jamaica Plain</a> area of Boston; the <a href="/wiki/Dumbarton_Oaks_Research_Library_and_Collection" title="Dumbarton Oaks Research Library and Collection" class="mw-redirect">Dumbarton Oaks Research Library and Collection</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Forest" title="Harvard Forest">Harvard Forest</a> in <a href="/wiki/Petersham,_Massachusetts" title="Petersham, Massachusetts">Petersham, Massachusetts</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Villa_I_Tatti" title="Villa I Tatti">Villa I Tatti</a> research center<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>. Harvard also operates the Harvard Shanghai Center in China.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_campus_expansion">Major campus expansion</span></h3> 
     <p>Harvard has purchased tracts of land in <a href="/wiki/Allston,_Boston,_Massachusetts" title="Allston, Boston, Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect">Allston</a>, a walk across the Charles River from Cambridge, with the intent of major expansion southward.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> The university now owns approximately fifty percent more land in Allston than in Cambridge. Proposals to connect the Cambridge campus with the new Allston campus include new and enlarged bridges, a shuttle service and/or a <a href="/wiki/Tram" title="Tram">tram</a>. Plans also call for sinking part of <a href="/wiki/Storrow_Drive" title="Storrow Drive">Storrow Drive</a> (at Harvard's expense) for replacement with park land and pedestrian access to the <a href="/wiki/Charles_River" title="Charles River">Charles River</a>, as well as the construction of bike paths, and buildings throughout the Allston campus. The institution asserts that such expansion will benefit not only the school, but surrounding community, pointing to such features as the enhanced transit infrastructure, possible shuttles open to the public, and park space which will also be publicly accessible.</p> 
     <p>One of the foremost driving forces for Harvard's pending expansion is its goal of increasing the scope and strength of its science and technology programs. The university plans to construct two 500,000 square foot (50,000 m&sup2;) research complexes in Allston, which would be home to several interdisciplinary programs, including the Harvard Stem Cell Institute and an enlarged <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">Engineering</a> department.</p> 
     <p>In addition, Harvard intends to relocate the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Graduate_School_of_Education" title="Harvard Graduate School of Education">Harvard Graduate School of Education</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_School_of_Public_Health" title="Harvard School of Public Health">Harvard School of Public Health</a> to Allston. The university also plans to construct several new undergraduate and graduate student housing centers in Allston, and it is considering large-scale museums and performing arts complexes as well. Unfortunately the large drop in endowment has halted these plans for now.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainability">Sustainability</span></h3> 
     <p>In 2000, Harvard hired a full-time campus <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a> professional and launched the Harvard Green Campus Initiative,<sup id="cite_ref-Harvard_Green_Campus_Initiative_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvard_Green_Campus_Initiative-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> since institutionalized as the Office for Sustainability (OFS).<sup id="cite_ref-Office_for_Sustainability_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Office_for_Sustainability-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>With a full-time staff of 25, dozens of student interns, and a $12 million Loan Fund for energy and water conservation projects, OFS is one of the most advanced campus sustainability programs in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-America.27s_Greenest_Colleges_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-America.27s_Greenest_Colleges-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Harvard was one of 27 schools to receive a grade of &quot;A-&quot; from the Sustainable Endowments Institute on its College Sustainability Report Card 2010, the highest grade awarded.<sup id="cite_ref-Sustainable_Endowments_Institute_Report_Card_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sustainable_Endowments_Institute_Report_Card-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization_and_administration">Organization and administration</span></h3>

     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Faculty_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Harvard Faculty of Arts and Sciences">Faculty of Arts and Sciences</a> has primary responsibility for instruction in <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Graduate_School_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Harvard Graduate School of Arts and Sciences">Graduate School of Arts and Sciences</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Division_of_Continuing_Education" title="Harvard Division of Continuing Education">Harvard Division of Continuing Education</a>, which includes <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Summer_School" title="Harvard Summer School">Harvard Summer School</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Extension_School" title="Harvard Extension School">Harvard Extension School</a>. There is also the <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_Institute_for_Advanced_Study" title="Radcliffe Institute for Advanced Study">Radcliffe Institute for Advanced Study</a>.</p> 
     <p>Harvard is governed by a combination<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2011)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of its <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Board_of_Overseers" title="Harvard Board of Overseers">Board of Overseers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/President_and_Fellows_of_Harvard_College" title="President and Fellows of Harvard College">President and Fellows of Harvard College</a> (also known as the Harvard Corporation), which in turn appoints the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Harvard_University" title="President of Harvard University">President of Harvard University</a>. There are 16,000 staff and faculty.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>A faculty of approximately 2,410 professors, lecturers, and instructors serve as of school year 2009?10,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> with 7,180 <a href="/wiki/Undergraduate" title="Undergraduate" class="mw-redirect">undergraduate</a> and 13,830 <a href="/wiki/Graduate_school" title="Graduate school">graduate</a> students.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> The school color is <a href="/wiki/Crimson" title="Crimson">crimson</a>, which is also the name of the Harvard sports teams and the daily newspaper, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Harvard_Crimson" title="The Harvard Crimson">The Harvard Crimson</a></i>. The color was unofficially adopted (in preference to <a href="/wiki/Magenta" title="Magenta">magenta</a>) by an 1875 vote of the student body, although the association with some form of red can be traced back to 1858, when <a href="/wiki/Charles_William_Eliot" title="Charles William Eliot">Charles William Eliot</a>, a young graduate student who would later become Harvard's 21st and longest-serving president (1869?1909), bought red bandanas for his crew so they could more easily be distinguished by spectators at a regatta.</p> 
     <p>Joint programs with the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Harvard-MIT_Division_of_Health_Sciences_and_Technology" title="Harvard-MIT Division of Health Sciences and Technology" class="mw-redirect">Harvard-MIT Division of Health Sciences and Technology</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Broad_Institute" title="Broad Institute">Broad Institute</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Observatory_of_Economic_Complexity" title="The Observatory of Economic Complexity">The Observatory of Economic Complexity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/EdX" title="EdX">edX</a>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_yard_winter_2009j.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Harvard_yard_winter_2009j.JPG/220px-Harvard_yard_winter_2009j.JPG" width="220" height="103" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Harvard_yard_winter_2009j.JPG/330px-Harvard_yard_winter_2009j.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Harvard_yard_winter_2009j.JPG/440px-Harvard_yard_winter_2009j.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_yard_winter_2009j.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Harvard Yard in the winter, with 
        <a href="/wiki/List_of_Harvard_dormitories" title="List of Harvard dormitories">freshman dorms</a> in the background
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Endowment">Endowment</span></h3> 
     <p>Harvard has the largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_colleges_and_universities_in_the_United_States_by_endowment" title="List of colleges and universities in the United States by endowment">university endowment</a> in the world. As of September 2011, it had nearly regained the loss suffered during the 2008 recession. It was worth $32 billion in 2011, up from $27.6 billion in September 2010<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> and $25.7 billion 2009. It suffered about 30% loss in 2008-2009.<sup id="cite_ref-NACUBO_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NACUBO-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> In December 2008, Harvard announced that its endowment had lost 22% (approximately $8 billion) from July to October 2008, necessitating budget cuts.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Later reports<sup id="cite_ref-vanity_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanity-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> suggest the loss was actually more than double that figure, a reduction of nearly 50% of its endowment in the first four months alone. Forbes in March 2009 estimated the loss to be in the range of $12 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-ForbesUndEnd_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ForbesUndEnd-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> One of the most visible results of Harvard's attempt to re-balance its budget was their halting<sup id="cite_ref-vanity_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanity-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> of construction of the $1.2 billion Allston Science Complex that had been scheduled to be completed by 2011, resulting in protests from local residents.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2012, Harvard University had a total financial aid reserve of $158.725 million for students, and a Pell Grant reserve of $4.093 million available for disbursement.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Massachusetts_Hall,_Harvard_University.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Massachusetts_Hall%2C_Harvard_University.JPG/220px-Massachusetts_Hall%2C_Harvard_University.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Massachusetts_Hall%2C_Harvard_University.JPG/330px-Massachusetts_Hall%2C_Harvard_University.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Massachusetts_Hall%2C_Harvard_University.JPG/440px-Massachusetts_Hall%2C_Harvard_University.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Massachusetts_Hall,_Harvard_University.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Hall,_Harvard_University" title="Massachusetts Hall, Harvard University" class="mw-redirect">Massachusetts Hall</a> (1720) is the oldest building in the Harvard campus.
        <sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Harvard is a large, highly residential research university.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> The university has been <a href="/wiki/Accreditation" title="Accreditation">accredited</a> by the <a href="/wiki/New_England_Association_of_Schools_and_Colleges" title="New England Association of Schools and Colleges">New England Association of Schools and Colleges</a> since 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> The university offers 46 undergraduate concentrations (majors),<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> 134 graduate degrees,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> and 32 professional degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-Degrees_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Degrees-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> For the 2008?2009 academic year, Harvard granted 1,664 baccalaureate degrees, 400 masters degrees, 512 doctoral degrees, and 4,460 professional degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-Degrees_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Degrees-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The four year, full-time undergraduate program comprises a minority of enrollments at the university and emphasizes instruction with an &quot;arts and sciences focus&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Between 1978 and 2008, entering students were required to complete a core curriculum of seven classes outside of their concentration.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Since 2008, undergraduate students have been required to complete courses in eight General Education categories: Aesthetic and Interpretive Understanding, Culture and Belief, Empirical and Mathematical Reasoning, Ethical Reasoning, Science of Living Systems, Science of the Physical Universe, Societies of the World, and United States in the World.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Harvard offers a comprehensive doctoral graduate program and there is a high level of coexistence between graduate and undergraduate degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Carnegie_Foundation_for_the_Advancement_of_Teaching" title="The Carnegie Foundation for the Advancement of Teaching" class="mw-redirect">The Carnegie Foundation for the Advancement of Teaching</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, and some students have criticized Harvard for its reliance on <a href="/wiki/Teaching_assistant" title="Teaching assistant">teaching fellows</a> for some aspects of undergraduate education; they consider this to adversely affect the quality of education.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Harvard's academic programs operate on a <a href="/wiki/Semester#Collegiate_calendars" title="Semester" class="mw-redirect">semester calendar</a> beginning in early September and ending in mid-May.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Undergraduates typically take four half-courses per term and must maintain a four-course rate average to be considered full-time.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> In many concentrations, students can elect to pursue a basic program or an honors-eligible program requiring a senior thesis and/or advanced course work.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Students graduating in the top 4?5% of the class are awarded degrees <i>summa cum laude</i>, students in the next 15% of the class are awarded <i>magna cum laude</i>, and the next 30% of the class are awarded <i>cum laude</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> Harvard has chapters of academic honor societies such as <a href="/wiki/Phi_Beta_Kappa" title="Phi Beta Kappa" class="mw-redirect">Phi Beta Kappa</a> and various committees and departments also award several hundred named prizes annually.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> Harvard, along with other universities, has been accused of <a href="/wiki/Grade_inflation" title="Grade inflation">grade inflation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> although there is evidence that the quality of the student body and its motivation have also increased.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Harvard College reduced the number of students who receive <a href="/wiki/Latin_honors" title="Latin honors">Latin honors</a> from 90% in 2004 to 60% in 2005. Moreover, the honors of &quot;John Harvard Scholar&quot; and &quot;Harvard College Scholar&quot; will now be given only to the top 5 percent and the next 5 percent of each class.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Undergraduate tuition for the 2009?2010 school year was $33,696 and the total cost with fees, room, and board was $48,868.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuition_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuition-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Under financial aid guidelines adopted in 2007, parents in families with incomes of less than $60,000 will no longer be expected to contribute any money to the cost of attending Harvard for their children, including room and board. Families with incomes in the $60,000 to $80,000 range contribute an amount of only a few thousand dollars a year. In December 2007, Harvard announced that families earning between $120,000 and $180,000 will only have to pay up to 10% of their annual household income towards tuition.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2009, Harvard offered grants totaling $414.1 million across all 11 divisions; $339.5 million came from institutional funds, $35.3 million from federal support, and $39.2 million from other outside support. Grants total 87.7% of Harvard's aid for undergraduate students, with aid also provided by loans (8.4%) and work-study (3.9%).<sup id="cite_ref-Tuition_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuition-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="University_rankings">University rankings</span></h3> 
     <table class="infobox vcard" style="width: 22em"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr>

       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th colspan="2" style="background-color: lavender; text-align: center">National</th> 
       </tr> 
       <tr>

        <td>11</td> 
       </tr>

       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th><i><a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">ARWU</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_ARWU_W_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_ARWU_W-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></th> 
        <td>1</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th><i><a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></th> 
        <td>3</td> 
       </tr> 
       <tr> 
        <th><i><a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">Times</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Rankings_THES_W_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankings_THES_W-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></th> 
        <td>4</td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p>Harvard has been highly ranked by many international university rankings. It has topped the league table of the <i><a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">Academic Ranking of World Universities</a></i> (<i>ARWU</i>) in 2012, a position held since the first ARWU rankings were released in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-ARWU_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARWU-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Meanwhile, it ranked third in the <i><a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS World University Rankings</a></i>, which is collaborating with <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i> to release its annual global ranking.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">Times Higher Education World University Rankings</a></i> of the same academic year considered it to be 4th<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> while its <i><a href="/wiki/World_Reputation_Rankings" title="World Reputation Rankings" class="mw-redirect">World Reputation Rankings</a></i>, which is based on a survey of academics around the world and is a spin-off of the main world rankings, has still ranked Harvard the best since 2011 when it was released for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> When the QS and Times were published in partnership as the <i><a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education-QS_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education-QS World University Rankings" class="mw-redirect">Times Higher Education-QS World University Rankings</a></i> during 2004-2009, Harvard was continuously regarded the first.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The University's undergraduate program is ranked number one among national universities by <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i> together with <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> sixth by <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> and 11th by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Monthly" title="The Washington Monthly" class="mw-redirect">The Washington Monthly</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In the 2009 <i><a href="/wiki/QS_Global_200_Business_Schools_Report" title="QS Global 200 Business Schools Report">QS Global 200 Business Schools Report</a></i>, Harvard was ranked first in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2012, Harvard ranked first in a University Ranking by Academic Performance (URAP).<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

            US. News Best Graduate Schools?2014

     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libraries_and_museums">Libraries and museums</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Widener_library_2009.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Widener_library_2009.JPG/220px-Widener_library_2009.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Widener_library_2009.JPG/330px-Widener_library_2009.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Widener_library_2009.JPG/440px-Widener_library_2009.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Widener_library_2009.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Widener_Library" title="Widener Library">Harry Elkins Widener</a> Memorial Library
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Library" title="Harvard University Library">Harvard University Library</a> System is centered in <a href="/wiki/Widener_Library" title="Widener Library">Widener Library</a> in <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Yard" title="Harvard Yard">Harvard Yard</a> and comprises over 80 individual libraries holding some 15 million volumes.<sup id="cite_ref-lib_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lib-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/American_Library_Association" title="American Library Association">American Library Association</a>, this makes it the largest academic library in the United States, and one of the largest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-largestlibs_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-largestlibs-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Cabot Science Library, Lamont Library, and Widener Library are three of the most popular libraries for undergraduates to use, with easy access and central locations. There are rare books, <a href="/wiki/Manuscripts" title="Manuscripts" class="mw-redirect">manuscripts</a> and other special collections throughout Harvard's libraries;<sup id="cite_ref-specialcolls_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-specialcolls-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Houghton Library, the Arthur and Elizabeth Schlesinger Library on the History of Women in America, and the Harvard University Archives consist principally of rare and unique materials. America's oldest collection of maps, gazetteers, and atlases both old and new is stored in Pusey Library and open to the public. The largest collection of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East-Asian</a> language material outside of East Asia is held in the <a href="/wiki/Harvard-Yenching_Library" title="Harvard-Yenching Library" class="mw-redirect">Harvard-Yenching Library</a>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Moore_sculpture,_Harvard_University.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Henry_Moore_sculpture%2C_Harvard_University.jpg/220px-Henry_Moore_sculpture%2C_Harvard_University.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Henry_Moore_sculpture%2C_Harvard_University.jpg/330px-Henry_Moore_sculpture%2C_Harvard_University.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Henry_Moore_sculpture%2C_Harvard_University.jpg/440px-Henry_Moore_sculpture%2C_Harvard_University.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Moore_sculpture,_Harvard_University.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moore" title="Henry Moore">Henry Moore</a>'s sculpture 
        <i>Large Four Piece Reclining Figure</i> near Lamont Library
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Harvard operates several arts, cultural, and scientific museums:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>The <a href="/wiki/Carpenter_Center_for_the_Visual_Arts" title="Carpenter Center for the Visual Arts">Carpenter Center for the Visual Arts</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/Le_Corbusier" title="Le Corbusier">Le Corbusier</a>, is home to the University's film archive and the department of Visual and Environmental Studies.</li> 
      <li>The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Art_Museums" title="Harvard Art Museums">Harvard Art Museums</a>, including: 
       <ul> 
        <li>The <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Sackler_Museum" title="Arthur M. Sackler Museum">Arthur M. Sackler Museum</a>, which includes ancient, Asian, Islamic and later Indian art</li> 
        <li>The <a href="/wiki/Busch-Reisinger_Museum" title="Busch-Reisinger Museum" class="mw-redirect">Busch-Reisinger Museum</a>, formerly the Germanic Museum, covers central and northern European art.</li> 
        <li>The <a href="/wiki/Fogg_Museum_of_Art" title="Fogg Museum of Art" class="mw-redirect">Fogg Museum of Art</a>, with galleries featuring history of Western art from the Middle Ages to the present. Particular strengths are in Italian <a href="/wiki/Early_Renaissance_painting" title="Early Renaissance painting" class="mw-redirect">early Renaissance</a>, British <a href="/wiki/Pre-Raphaelite" title="Pre-Raphaelite" class="mw-redirect">pre-Raphaelite</a>, and 19th century French art</li> 
       </ul> </li> 
      <li>The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Harvard Museum of Natural History">Harvard Museum of Natural History</a> complex, including: 
       <ul> 
        <li>The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Mineralogical_Museum" title="Harvard Mineralogical Museum">Harvard Mineralogical Museum</a></li> 
        <li>The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Herbaria" title="Harvard University Herbaria">Harvard University Herbaria</a>, which contains the famous <a href="/wiki/Leopold_and_Rudolf_Blaschka" title="Leopold and Rudolf Blaschka">Blaschka</a> <a href="/wiki/Glass_Flowers" title="Glass Flowers">Glass Flowers</a> exhibit</li> 
        <li>The <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Comparative_Zoology" title="Museum of Comparative Zoology">Museum of Comparative Zoology</a></li> 
       </ul> </li> 
      <li>The <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Museum_of_Archaeology_and_Ethnology" title="Peabody Museum of Archaeology and Ethnology">Peabody Museum of Archaeology and Ethnology</a>, specializing in the cultural history and civilizations of the Western Hemisphere</li> 
      <li>The <a href="/wiki/Semitic_Museum" title="Semitic Museum">Semitic Museum</a></li> 
     </ul> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tradition_and_culture">Tradition and culture</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Athletics">Athletics</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Crimson" title="Harvard Crimson">Harvard Crimson</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_stadium_2009h.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Harvard_stadium_2009h.JPG/220px-Harvard_stadium_2009h.JPG" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Harvard_stadium_2009h.JPG/330px-Harvard_stadium_2009h.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Harvard_stadium_2009h.JPG/440px-Harvard_stadium_2009h.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_stadium_2009h.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Stadium" title="Harvard Stadium">Harvard Stadium</a>, home of 
        <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Crimson" title="Harvard Crimson">Harvard Crimson</a> and the 
        <a href="/wiki/Boston_Cannons" title="Boston Cannons">Boston Cannons</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_Rowing_Crew_at_Henley_2004_-2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Harvard_Rowing_Crew_at_Henley_2004_-2.JPG/220px-Harvard_Rowing_Crew_at_Henley_2004_-2.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Harvard_Rowing_Crew_at_Henley_2004_-2.JPG/330px-Harvard_Rowing_Crew_at_Henley_2004_-2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Harvard_Rowing_Crew_at_Henley_2004_-2.JPG/440px-Harvard_Rowing_Crew_at_Henley_2004_-2.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_Rowing_Crew_at_Henley_2004_-2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Harvard men's eight crew at 
        <a href="/wiki/Henley_Royal_Regatta" title="Henley Royal Regatta">Henley</a>, 2004
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Bright_Hockey_Center,_Harvard.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Bright_Hockey_Center%2C_Harvard.JPG/220px-Bright_Hockey_Center%2C_Harvard.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Bright_Hockey_Center%2C_Harvard.JPG/330px-Bright_Hockey_Center%2C_Harvard.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Bright_Hockey_Center%2C_Harvard.JPG/440px-Bright_Hockey_Center%2C_Harvard.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Bright_Hockey_Center,_Harvard.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Cornell%E2%80%93Harvard_hockey_rivalry" title="Cornell?Harvard hockey rivalry">Cornell?Harvard hockey rivalry match</a>, 2006
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Harvard's athletic rivalry with <a href="/wiki/Yale_Bulldogs" title="Yale Bulldogs">Yale</a> is intense in every sport in which they meet, coming to a climax each fall in the annual <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">football</a> meeting, which dates back to 1875 and is usually called simply &quot;<a href="/wiki/Harvard%E2%80%93Yale_football_rivalry" title="Harvard?Yale football rivalry">The Game</a>&quot;. While Harvard's football team is no longer one of the country's best as it often was a century ago during football's early days (it won the <a href="/wiki/Rose_Bowl_Game" title="Rose Bowl Game">Rose Bowl</a> in 1920), both it and Yale have influenced the way the game is played. In 1903, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Stadium" title="Harvard Stadium">Harvard Stadium</a> introduced a new era into football with the first-ever permanent reinforced concrete stadium of its kind in the country. The stadium's structure actually played a role in the evolution of the college game. Seeking to reduce the alarming number of deaths and serious injuries in the sport, the Father of Football, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Camp" title="Walter Camp">Walter Camp</a> (former captain of the Yale football team), suggested widening the field to open up the game. But the stadium was too narrow to accommodate a wider playing surface. So, other steps had to be taken. Camp would instead support revolutionary new rules for the 1906 season. These included legalizing the <a href="/wiki/Forward_pass" title="Forward pass">forward pass</a>, perhaps the most significant rule change in the sport's history.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Harvard has several athletic facilities, such as the <a href="/wiki/Lavietes_Pavilion" title="Lavietes Pavilion">Lavietes Pavilion</a>, a multi-purpose arena and home to the Harvard basketball teams. The Malkin Athletic Center, known as the &quot;MAC&quot;, serves both as the university's primary recreation facility and as a satellite location for several varsity sports. The five story building includes two cardio rooms, an <a href="/wiki/Olympic-size_swimming_pool" title="Olympic-size swimming pool">Olympic-size swimming pool</a>, a smaller pool for aquaerobics and other activities, a mezzanine, where all types of classes are held, an indoor cycling studio, three weight rooms, and a three-court gym floor to play basketball. The MAC offers personal trainers and specialty classes. The MAC is home to Harvard volleyball, fencing, and wrestling. The offices of several of the school's varsity coaches are also in the MAC.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Weld_Boathouse" title="Weld Boathouse">Weld Boathouse</a> and Newell Boathouse house the women's and men's rowing teams, respectively. The men's crew also uses the Red Top complex in Ledyard, Connecticut, as their training camp for the annual <a href="/wiki/Harvard-Yale_Regatta" title="Harvard-Yale Regatta" class="mw-redirect">Harvard-Yale Regatta</a>. The Bright Hockey Center hosts the Harvard hockey teams, and the Murr Center serves both as a home for Harvard's squash and tennis teams as well as a strength and conditioning center for all athletic sports.</p> 
     <p>As of 2013, there were 42 Division I intercollegiate <a href="/wiki/Varsity_team" title="Varsity team">varsity</a> sports teams for women and men at Harvard, more than at any other <a href="/wiki/Division_I_(NCAA)" title="Division I (NCAA)">NCAA Division I</a> college in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> As with other Ivy League universities, Harvard does not offer <a href="/wiki/Athletic_scholarship" title="Athletic scholarship">athletic scholarships</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Older than <i>The Game</i> by 23 years, the Harvard-Yale Regatta was the original source of the athletic rivalry between the two schools. It is held annually in June on the <a href="/wiki/Thames_River_(Connecticut)" title="Thames River (Connecticut)">Thames River</a> in eastern Connecticut. The Harvard crew is typically considered to be one of the top teams in the country in <a href="/wiki/Rowing_(sport)" title="Rowing (sport)">rowing</a>. Today, Harvard fields top teams in several other sports, such as the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Crimson_men%27s_ice_hockey" title="Harvard Crimson men's ice hockey">Harvard Crimson men's ice hockey</a> team (with a strong rivalry against <a href="/wiki/Cornell_Big_Red_men%27s_ice_hockey" title="Cornell Big Red men's ice hockey">Cornell</a>), <a href="/wiki/Squash_(sport)" title="Squash (sport)">squash</a>, and even recently won NCAA titles in Men's and Women's <a href="/wiki/Fencing" title="Fencing">Fencing</a>. Harvard also won the <a href="/wiki/Intercollegiate_Sailing_Association_National_Championships" title="Intercollegiate Sailing Association National Championships">Intercollegiate Sailing Association National Championships</a> in 2003.</p> 
     <p>Harvard's men's ice hockey team won the school's first NCAA Championship in any team sport in 1989. Harvard was also the first Ivy League institution to win a NCAA championship title in a women's sport when its women's lacrosse team won the NCAA Championship in 1990.</p> 
     <p><i><a href="/wiki/Harvard_Undergraduate_Television" title="Harvard Undergraduate Television">Harvard Undergraduate Television</a></i> has footage from historical games and athletic events including the 2005 pep-rally before the Harvard-Yale Game.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Song">Song</span></h3> 
     <p>Harvard has several fight songs, the most played of which, especially at football, are &quot;<a href="/wiki/Ten_Thousand_Men_of_Harvard" title="Ten Thousand Men of Harvard">Ten Thousand Men of Harvard</a>&quot; and &quot;<a href="/wiki/Harvardiana" title="Harvardiana">Harvardiana</a>.&quot; While &quot;<a href="/wiki/Fair_Harvard" title="Fair Harvard">Fair Harvard</a>&quot; is actually the <a href="/wiki/Alma_mater" title="Alma mater">alma mater</a>, &quot;Ten Thousand Men&quot; is better known outside the university. The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Band" title="Harvard University Band">Harvard University Band</a> performs these fight songs, and other cheers, at football and hockey games. These were parodied by Harvard alumnus <a href="/wiki/Tom_Lehrer" title="Tom Lehrer">Tom Lehrer</a> in his song &quot;<a href="/wiki/Fight_Fiercely,_Harvard" title="Fight Fiercely, Harvard">Fight Fiercely, Harvard</a>,&quot; which he composed while an undergraduate.</p>
